href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStanley200896-56">[56]</a></sup> For Laclau, socialism was "the highest form of populism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarch200765_134-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarch200765-134">[134]</a></sup> His understandings of the topic derived in large part from his focus on politics in Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETormey2018262_135-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTETormey2018262-135">[135]</a></sup> This definition is popular among critics of [liberal democracy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberal_democracy "Liberal democracy") and is widely used in critical studies and in studies of West European and Latin American politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20173_133-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20173-133">[133]</a></sup> Harry C. Boyte for example defined populism as "a politics of civic agency" which "develops the power of 'the people' to shape their destiny", as examples citing both the Russian narodniks and the South African [Black Consciousness Movement](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Consciousness_Movement "Black Consciousness Movement").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyte2012173_136-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyte2012173-136">[136]</a></sup>

The socioeconomic definition of populism applies the term to what it regards as an irresponsible form of economic policy by which a government engages in a period of massive public spending financed by foreign loans, after which the country falls into [hyperinflation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperinflation "Hyperinflation") and harsh economic adjustments are then imposed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20173–4HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196_137-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20173%E2%80%934HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196-137">[137]</a></sup> This use of the term was used by economists like [Rudiger Dornbusch](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rudiger_Dornbusch "Rudiger Dornbusch") and [Jeffrey Sachs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeffrey_Sachs "Jeffrey Sachs") and was particularly popular among scholars of Latin America during the 1980s and 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20173_133-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20173-133">[133]</a></sup> Since that time, this definition continued to be used by some economists and journalists, particularly in the US, but was uncommon among other [social sciences](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_sciences "Social sciences").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20174_138-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20174-138">[138]</a></sup> This definition relies on focusing on socialist and other left-wing forms of populism; it does not apply to other groups commonly understood as populist which adopted right-wing stances on economic issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196_139-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196-139">[139]</a></sup>

An additional framework has been described as the "political-strategic" approach.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196_139-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196-139">[139]</a></sup> This applies the term _populism_ to a political strategy in which a charismatic leader seeks to govern based on direct and unmediated connection with their followers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20174HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196_140-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20174HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196-140">[140]</a></sup> [Kurt Weyland](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kurt_Weyland&action=edit&redlink=1 "Kurt Weyland (page does not exist)") defined this conception of _populism_ as "a political strategy through which a personalist leader seeks or exercises government power based on direct, unmediated, uninstitutionalized support from large numbers of mostly unorganized followers".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2013153–154HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196_141-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2013153%E2%80%93154HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196-141">[141]</a></sup> This is a definition of the term that is popular among scholars of non-Western societies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20174_138-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser20174-138">[138]</a></sup> By focusing on leadership, this concept of _populism_ does not allow for the existence of populist parties or populist social movements;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196_139-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser20196-139">[139]</a></sup> under this definition, for instance, the US People's Party which first invented the term _populism_ could not be considered populist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2013154_142-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2013154-142">[142]</a></sup> Mudde suggested that although the idea of a leader having direct access to "the people" was a common element among populists, it is best regarded as a feature which facilitates rather than defines populism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004545_143-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004545-143">[143]</a></sup>

In popular discourse, _populism_ is sometimes used in a negative sense in reference to politics which involves promoting extremely simple solutions to complex problems in a highly emotional manner.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542_144-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542-144">[144]</a></sup> Mudde suggested that this definition "seems to have instinctive value" but was difficult to employ empirically because almost all political groups engage in sloganeering and because it can be difficult to differentiate an argument made emotionally from one made rationally.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542_144-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542-144">[144]</a></sup> Mudde thought that this phenomenon was better termed _[demagogy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demagogy "Demagogy")_ rather than _populism_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004543_44-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004543-44">[44]</a></sup> Another use of the term in popular discourse is to describe opportunistic policies designed to quickly please voters rather than deciding a more rational course of action.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542_144-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542-144">[144]</a></sup> Examples of this would include a governing political party lowering taxes before an election or promising to provide things to the electorate which the state cannot afford to pay for.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542–43_145-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542%E2%80%9343-145">[145]</a></sup> Mudde suggested that this phenomenon is better described as _[opportunism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_opportunism "Political opportunism")_ rather than _populism_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542_144-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004542-144">[144]</a></sup>

Another way of defining populism is by defining it as a political style. Moffitt states that political style can be defined as “the repertoires of embodied, symbolically mediated performance made to audiences that are used to create and navigate the fields of power that comprise the political, stretching from the domain of government through to everyday life.” This definition acknowledges that populism includes both rhetorical aspects such as gestures and body language, spoken language, argumentation while also acknowledging that populism includes aesthetic aspects such as fashion, self-presentation, images, and designs. This definition also acknowledges that political performances are constructed. Moffitt argues that an ideational approach doesn’t include the emphasis of performative elements while political style does. In addition, populism cannot be considered an ideology because it doesn’t comprise of specific ideas or ideals related to economic or political theory and policy. Populism as a political style is only concerned with the way that political ideas are presented and preformed. According to Moffitt, this is why populism can appear across a number of different ideological spectrums on the left and right. Populism has no political ideology; it is only a political style.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_University_Press_146-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Stanford_University_Press-146">[146]</a></sup>

Moffitt notes that populism as a political style has certain features which define it. The first of these features is ‘the People’ versus ‘the Elite’. Moffitt acknowledges that the “dichotomic division of society between ‘the people’ and ‘the elite’” is one of the most popular definitions of populism but is only one of the features of populism as a political style. The second feature of populism as a political style is the use of ‘bad manners’. ‘Bad manners’ consists of, “slang, swearing, political incorrectness, and being over demonstrative and ‘colorful’, as opposed to the ‘high’ behaviors of rigidness, rationality, composure and use of technocratic language.” The third feature of populism as a political style is crisis, breakdown, or threat. This is the exigence that populists use. Moffitt writes that, “Crises are often related to the breakdown between citizens and their representatives, but can also be related to immigration, economic difficulties, perceived injustice, military threat, social change or other issues.” This performance relates to a distrust of deliberation, negotiation, consultations, reviews, reports, and the complicated nature of designing policy solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_University_Press_146-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Stanford_University_Press-146">[146]</a></sup>

There are multiple implications for populism being understood as a political style. The first implication that Moffitt points out is that populism as a political style allows people to understand why populism doesn’t adhere to the common left/right ideological spectrum. Populism is not a political ideology; it is just a way to present ideas through both rhetorical and aesthetic aspects. In addition, populism as a political style means that populism no longer has to be conceptualized as a binary category but can instead be conceptualized as a gradational concept. This means that populist actors can, depending on the time, be more or less populist. However, this means that there has to be an opposite political style to populism. Moffitt notes that the opposing political style to populism is a technocratic political style. In contrast with appeal to ‘the people’ vs. ‘the elite’, ‘bad manners’, and the exigence of a crisis, breakdown threat, the technocratic political style emphasizes appeal to expertise, ‘good manners’, and stability and progress. This distinction between these two political styles allows political actors to be plotted on a scale rather than being seen as populist or not. It also allows for the scale to be adjusted in relation to different political elections in different years due to the fact that populists might not always utilize the populist political style to the same extent as they did in years prior.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_University_Press_146-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Stanford_University_Press-146">[146]</a></sup>

## Demand-side factors [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Demand-side factors")

One area of debate in explaining populism is whether its main cause is based in the needs of citizens (demand-side explanations) or in the failures of governments (supply-side explanations). In focusing on the changing grievances or demands of citizens, demand-side explanations can be seen as bottom-up explanations, while supply-side explanations, in focusing on political actors and institutions, can be seen as top-down explanations.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup> Various demand-side factors have been claimed to make it more likely that individuals will support populist ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-147"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Economists and political economists often emphasize the importance of economic concerns while political scientists and sociologists often emphasize sociocultural concerns in their analysis of demand-side factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

### Economic grievance [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Economic grievance")

The economic grievance thesis argues that economic factors, such as [deindustrialisation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deindustrialization "Deindustrialization"), [economic liberalisation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_liberalization "Economic liberalization"), and [deregulation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deregulation "Deregulation"), are causing the formation of a 'left-behind' precariat with low [job security](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Job_security "Job security"), high [inequality](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_inequality "Economic inequality"), and [wage stagnation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wage_stagnation "Wage stagnation"), who then support populism.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_148-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:2-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Some theories only focus on the effect of [economic crises](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recession "Recession"),<sup id="cite_ref-150"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> or inequality.<sup id="cite_ref-151"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Another objection for economic reasons is due to the globalization that is taking place in the world today. In addition to criticism of the widening inequality caused by the elite, the widening inequality among the general public caused by the influx of immigrants and other factors due to globalization is also a target of populist criticism.

The evidence of increasing economic disparity and volatility of family incomes is clear, particularly in the United States, as shown by the work of [Thomas Piketty](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Piketty "Thomas Piketty") and others.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Piketty_152-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Piketty-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hacker_153-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Hacker-153">[153]</a></sup> Commentators such as [Martin Wolf](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Wolf "Martin Wolf") emphasize the importance of economics.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolf_154-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Wolf-154">[154]</a></sup> They warn that such trends increase resentment and make people susceptible to populist rhetoric. Evidence for this is mixed. At the macro level, political scientists report that xenophobia, anti-immigrant ideas, and resentment towards out-groups tend to be higher during difficult economic times.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dancygier_155-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Dancygier-155">[155]</a></sup> Economic crises have been associated with gains by far-right political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-156"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> However, there is little evidence at the micro- or individual level to link individual economic grievances and populist support.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_148-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:2-148">[148]</a></sup> Populist politicians tend to put pressure on [central bank independence](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_bank#Independence "Central bank").<sup id="cite_ref-158"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>

### Modernisation [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Modernisation")

The [modernisation losers theory](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modernisation_losers_thesis "Modernisation losers thesis") argues that certain aspects of transition to modernity have caused demand for populism.<sup id="cite_ref-159"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Some arguments rely on the belief that [anomie](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anomie "Anomie") has followed [industrialisation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrialisation "Industrialisation") and resulted in "dissolution, fragmentation and differentiation", weakening the traditional ties of [civil society](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_society "Civil society"), and increasing [individualization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Individualism "Individualism").<sup id="cite_ref-160"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Populism offers a broad identity which gives sovereignty to the previously marginalized masses as "the people".<sup id="cite_ref-161"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> However, empirical studies suggest that supporters of radical right-wing populism occur across the social spectrum, and are not more likely to appear in groups defined as "modernisation losers".<sup id="cite_ref-Pereyra_Doval_162-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Pereyra_Doval-162">[162]</a></sup>

### Cultural backlash [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Cultural backlash")

Other theories argue that grievances have a primarily sociocultural rather than an economic basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup> For example, the cultural backlash thesis argues that right-wing populism is reaction to the rise of [postmaterialism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postmaterialism "Postmaterialism") in many [developed countries](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developed_country "Developed country"), including the spread of [feminism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feminism "Feminism"), [multiculturalism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiculturalism "Multiculturalism"), and [environmentalism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmentalism "Environmentalism").<sup id="cite_ref-Norris_Inglehart_2019_163-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Norris_Inglehart_2019-163">[163]</a></sup> According to this view, the spread of ideas and values through a society challenges accepted norms until society reaches a 'tipping point', which causes a reaction, in this case support for right-wing populism.<sup id="cite_ref-Norris_Inglehart_2019_163-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Norris_Inglehart_2019-163">[163]</a></sup> Some theories limit this argument to being a reaction to just the increase of ethnic diversity from [immigration](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration "Immigration").<sup id="cite_ref-Mudde_2007_164-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Mudde_2007-164">[164]</a></sup> Such theories are particularly popular with sociologists and with political scientists studying industrial world and American politics.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

The empiric studies testing this theory have produced highly contradicting results.<sup id="cite_ref-Mudde_2007_164-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Mudde_2007-164">[164]</a></sup> At the micro- or individual level, there are strong connections between individual positions on sociocultural issues (such as immigration policy and "racial animus") and right-wing populist voting. However, at the macro level, studies have not shown clear relationships between measures of populist sentiment in countries and actual right-wing party support.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

However, there is strong evidence from political scientists and political psychologists documenting the influence of group-based identity threats on voters. Those who identify as part of a group and perceive it as being under threat are likely to support political actors who promise to protect the status and identity of their group.<sup id="cite_ref-Craig_165-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Craig-165">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mutz_166-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Mutz-166">[166]</a></sup> While such research often focuses on white identity, results apply broadly to other social groups that perceive themselves to be under threat.<sup id="cite_ref-Outten_167-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Outten-167">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Taijfel_168-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Taijfel-168">[168]</a></sup>

### Recent democratization [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Recent democratization")

The length of time since a country has been [democratized](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratization "Democratization") has also been linked to its potential for populist success. This is claimed to be because younger democracies have less established political parties and weaker liberal democratic norms.<sup id="cite_ref-169"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> For example, populist success in [Eastern Europe](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe") has been linked to the legacy of [communism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communism "Communism").<sup id="cite_ref-170"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> However, this explanation suffers from the lack of success of populism in most [post-communist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-communism "Post-communism") countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Mudde_2007_164-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Mudde_2007-164">[164]</a></sup>

## Supply-side factors [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Supply-side factors")

Supply-side explanations focus on political actors and institutions and the ways in which governments may fail to respond to the changing conditions that affect citizens. Economic, social, and other structural trends are seen as being modified by institutions as they determine political outcomes. In this view, citizens turn to populism when governments do not respond effectively to the challenges they and their citizens face.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steinmo_171-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Steinmo-171">[171]</a></sup> Research supports the idea that populism is more likely to thrive when mainstream parties on the center-left and center-right do not address important contemporary issues and do not offer clear alternatives to voters. Coalitions that blur distinctions on positions are also likely to increase populism.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

> Economic and/or social changes alone are not problems—they only cause citizens to become angry, resentful, and susceptible to the appeal of populists if established mainstream politicians, parties, and governments fail to recognize and respond to them.

Sheri Berman<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

In _[Political Order in Changing Societies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_Order_in_Changing_Societies "Political Order in Changing Societies")_ (1968), [Samuel P. Huntington](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samuel_P._Huntington "Samuel P. Huntington") argues that rapid change (social or economic) in a society will increase the demands of its citizens. Unless political institutions are responsive and effective, they are unlikely to respond to and satisfy such demands. If political systems are weak or have become unresponsive over time, then dissatisfaction, political disorder and even violence become more likely. Political institutions that do not respond to social and economic changes are likely to fail. Responsive political systems can adapt to more severe challenges than unresponsive ones. Huntington's ideas grew out of work on [Third World](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_World "Third World") countries, but are also applicable to advanced industrial countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Huntington_172-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Huntington-172">[172]</a></sup>

In a supply-side view of American politics, populism can be seen as a symptom of institutional decay. It can be suggested that political factors such as [gerrymandering](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerrymandering "Gerrymandering"), the [Electoral College](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Electoral_College "United States Electoral College"), special-interest [lobbying](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lobbying_in_the_United_States "Lobbying in the United States") and [dark money](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_money "Dark money"), are distorting political and economic debate, and decreasing the ability of the government to respond to the concerns of large numbers of citizens. This in turn generates dissatisfaction, which may increase the likelihood that citizens will support populism. Scholars studying the [European Union](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") have suggested that European integration may have had the undesired effect of decreasing the system's responsiveness to voters, as law and policy-making increasingly became the responsibility of the European Union. This too may have increased support for populism.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup> Institutions such as the [European Central Bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Central_Bank "European Central Bank") may also distance decision-making from electoral power.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker_173-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Tucker-173">[173]</a></sup> It has been argued that political parties themselves have become disconnected from society, and unable to respond to citizen's concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-Mair_174-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Mair-174">[174]</a></sup>

## Voluntarism [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Voluntarism")

Another underlying debate in discussions of populism is the comparison of structural and voluntarist approaches. Voluntarist or agency-based explanations focus on the behaviors of politicians and parties, including populists themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

An important area of research is the examination of how parties develop, and how responses to new parties shape them. Successful politicians and parties shape the formation of agendas, identifying and increasing the salience of issues which they believe will benefit them.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-15"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

Established parties may adopt various strategies when a new party appears: dismissive, adversarial, or accommodative. A dismissive strategy such as ignoring a party and its issue(s) can only be effective if the issue involved is unimportant or short-lived. Otherwise, dismissing an issue leaves ownership of the issue with the new party and allows them to attract any voters who see the issue as important. In an adversarial response, a mainstream party directly engages over an issue, emphasizing their opposition to the new party's position. This increases the issue's visibility, makes it a focus of ongoing political debate, and can reinforce the new party's ownership of it.<sup id="cite_ref-Meguid_175-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Meguid-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bonnie_176-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Bonnie-176">[176]</a></sup>

An adversarial response can be to the benefit of a mainstream party if most voters, or at least the mainstream party's voters, disagree with the new party's position and are unlikely to ally with it as a result. An accommodative strategy is to move the mainstream party closer to the position advocated by the new party, in hopes of retaining voters who care about the issue. This works best if adopted early, before a new party is heavily identified with an issue. If an issue is important, long-lived and of strong interest to its supporters, a mainstream party can benefit from quickly shifting its position to one closer to the new party.<sup id="cite_ref-Meguid_175-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Meguid-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bonnie_176-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Bonnie-176">[176]</a></sup>

Similarly, a populist party with neo-fascist or antidemocratic roots may be able to increase its support by moderating its views to a milder form of its original position (e.g. from neofascist to xenophobic.) Right-wing populists are more effective in mobilizing voters around issues when mainstream parties ignore the issue or offer alternatives that are not aligned with voter opinions. They are also more likely to benefit from emphasizing social and cultural issues such as immigration and race, appealing to voters who are positioned economically towards the left-wing but hold socially conservative views.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-16"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup>

## Mobilisation [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Mobilisation")

There are three forms of political mobilisation which populists have adopted: that of the populist leader, the populist political party, and the populist social movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201742–43GagnonBeausoleilSonArguelles2018vi_177-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201742%E2%80%9343GagnonBeausoleilSonArguelles2018vi-177">[177]</a></sup> The reasons why voters are attracted to populists differ, but common catalysts for the rise of populists include dramatic economic decline or a systematic corruption scandal that damages established political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017100_178-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017100-178">[178]</a></sup> For instance, the [Great Recession](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Recession "Great Recession") of 2007 and its impact on the economies of southern Europe was a catalyst for the rise of [Syriza](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syriza "Syriza") in Greece and [Podemos](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Podemos_(Spanish_political_party) "Podemos (Spanish political party)") in Spain, while the _[Mani pulite](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mani_pulite "Mani pulite")_ corruption scandal of the early 1990s played a significant part in the rise of the Italian populist [Silvio Berlusconi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi "Silvio Berlusconi").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017100_178-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017100-178">[178]</a></sup>

Another catalyst for the growth of populism is a widespread perception among voters that the political system is unresponsive to them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017101_179-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017101-179">[179]</a></sup> This can arise when elected governments introduce policies that are unpopular with their voters but which are implemented because they are considered to be "responsible" or imposed by supranational organisations. In Latin America, for example, many countries passed unpopular economic reforms under pressure from the [International Monetary Fund](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund "International Monetary Fund") and [World Bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank") while in Europe, many [countries in the European Union](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union "Member state of the European Union") were pushed to implement unpopular economic [austerity](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austerity "Austerity") measures by the union's authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017101–102_180-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017101%E2%80%93102-180">[180]</a></sup> Decentralisation of political power is a very useful tool for populists to use to their benefit, this is because it allows them to speak more directly to the people of whom they seek to gain attention and votes.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenny_2017_181-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Kenny_2017-181">[181]</a></sup>

### Leaders [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Leaders")

Populism is often associated with charismatic and dominant leaders,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETormey2018268_182-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTETormey2018268-182">[182]</a></sup> and the populist leader is, according to Mudde and Rovira Kaltwasser, "the quintessential form of populist mobilization".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743_183-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743-183">[183]</a></sup> These individuals campaign and attract support on the basis of their own personal appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743_183-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743-183">[183]</a></sup> Their supporters then develop a perceived personal connection with the leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743_183-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743-183">[183]</a></sup> For these leaders, populist rhetoric allows them to claim that they have a direct relationship with "the people",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201744_184-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201744-184">[184]</a></sup> and in many cases they claim to be a personification of "the people" themselves,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743de_la_Torre2017197_185-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743de_la_Torre2017197-185">[185]</a></sup> presenting themselves as the _vox populi_ or "voice of the people".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201762de_la_Torre2017204_186-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201762de_la_Torre2017204-186">[186]</a></sup> [Hugo Chávez](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez "Hugo Chávez") for instance stated: "I demand absolute loyalty to me. I am not an individual, I am the people."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_la_Torre2017202_187-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_la_Torre2017202-187">[187]</a></sup> Populist leaders can also present themselves as the saviour of the people because of their perceived unique talents and vision, and in doing so can claim to be making personal sacrifices for the good of the people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell20084_59-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell20084-59">[59]</a></sup> Because loyalty to the populist leader is thus seen as representing loyalty to the people, those who oppose the leader can be branded "enemies of the people".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell20087_188-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell20087-188">[188]</a></sup>

The overwhelming majority of populist leaders have been men,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743_183-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743-183">[183]</a></sup> although there have been various females occupying this role.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201769_189-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201769-189">[189]</a></sup> Most of these female populist leaders gained positions of seniority through their connections to previously dominant men; [Eva Perón](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eva_Per%C3%B3n "Eva Perón") was the wife of [Juan Perón](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juan_Per%C3%B3n "Juan Perón"), [Marine Le Pen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_Le_Pen "Marine Le Pen") the daughter of [Jean-Marie Le Pen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Marie_Le_Pen "Jean-Marie Le Pen"), [Keiko Fujimori](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keiko_Fujimori "Keiko Fujimori") the daughter of [Alberto Fujimori](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alberto_Fujimori "Alberto Fujimori"), and [Yingluck Shinawatra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yingluck_Shinawatra "Yingluck Shinawatra") the sister of [Thaksin Shinawatra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thaksin_Shinawatra "Thaksin Shinawatra").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774_190-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774-190">[190]</a></sup>

#### Rhetorical styles [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Rhetorical styles")

Populist leaders often play on gendered stereotypes. US-based [Sarah Palin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sarah_Palin "Sarah Palin") portrayed a maternal image as a "mama grizzly";<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770_191-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770-191">[191]</a></sup> Italy's [Silvio Berlusconi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi "Silvio Berlusconi") boasted of his sexual virility.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764_192-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764-192">[192]</a></sup>

Canovan noted that populists often used "colourful and undiplomatic language" to distinguish themselves from the governing elite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanovan2004242_193-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanovan2004242-193">[193]</a></sup> In Africa, several populist leaders have distinguished themselves by speaking in indigenous languages rather than either French or English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEResnick2017110_194-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEResnick2017110-194">[194]</a></sup> Populist leaders often present themselves as people of action rather than people of words, talking of the need for "bold action" and "common sense solutions" to issues which they call "crises".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764_192-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764-192">[192]</a></sup> Male populist leaders often express themselves using simple and sometimes vulgar language in an attempt to present themselves as "the common man" or "one of the boys" to add to their populist appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764,_66_195-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764,_66-195">[195]</a></sup>

An example of this is [Umberto Bossi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umberto_Bossi "Umberto Bossi"), the leader of the right-wing populist Italian [Lega Nord](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lega_Nord "Lega Nord"), who at rallies would state "the League has a hard-on" while [putting his middle-finger](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_finger "The finger") up as a sign of disrespect to the government in Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201766_196-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201766-196">[196]</a></sup> Another recurring feature of male populist leaders is the emphasis that they place on their own virility.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764_192-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764-192">[192]</a></sup> An example of this is the Italian Prime Minister [Silvio Berlusconi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi "Silvio Berlusconi"), who bragged about his [bunga bunga](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bunga_bunga "Bunga bunga") sex parties and his ability to seduce young women.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764_192-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201764-192">[192]</a></sup> Among female populist leaders, it is more common for them to emphasise their role as a wife and mother.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770_191-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770-191">[191]</a></sup> The US right-wing populist [Sarah Palin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sarah_Palin "Sarah Palin") for instance referred to herself as a "hockey mom" and a "mama grizzly",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770_191-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770-191">[191]</a></sup> while Australian right-wing populist [Pauline Hanson](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pauline_Hanson "Pauline Hanson") stated that "I care so passionately about this country, it's like I'm its mother. Australia is my home and the Australian people are my children."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770_191-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770-191">[191]</a></sup>

Populist leaders typically portray themselves as outsiders who are separate from the "elite". Female populist leaders sometimes reference their gender as setting them apart from the dominant "old boys' club",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201769–70_197-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201769%E2%80%9370-197">[197]</a></sup> while in Latin America a number of populists, such as Evo Morales and Alberto Fujimori, emphasised their non-white ethnic background to set them apart from the white-dominated elite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201772–73de_la_Torre2017198_198-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201772%E2%80%9373de_la_Torre2017198-198">[198]</a></sup> Other populists have used clothing to set them apart.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEResnick2017110_194-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEResnick2017110-194">[194]</a></sup> In South Africa, the populist [Julius Malema](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julius_Malema "Julius Malema") and members of his [Economic Freedom Fighters](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_Freedom_Fighters "Economic Freedom Fighters") attended parliament dressed as miners and workers to distinguish themselves from the other politicians wearing suits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEResnick2017110_194-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEResnick2017110-194">[194]</a></sup> In instances where wealthy business figures promote populist sentiments, such as [Ross Perot](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ross_Perot "Ross Perot"), Thaksin Shinawatra, or Berlusconi, it can be difficult to present themselves as being outside the elite, however this is achieved by portraying themselves as being apart from the political, if not the economic elite, and portraying themselves as reluctant politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770–71_199-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201770%E2%80%9371-199">[199]</a></sup> Mudde and Rovira Kaltwasser noted that "in reality, most populist leaders are very much part of the national elite", typically being highly educated, upper-middle class, middle-aged males from the majority ethnicity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201773–74_200-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201773%E2%80%9374-200">[200]</a></sup>

Mudde and Rovira Kaltwasser suggested that "true outsiders" to the political system are rare, although cited instances like Venezuela's Chávez and Peru's Fujimori.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774–75_201-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774%E2%80%9375-201">[201]</a></sup> More common is that they are "insider-outsiders", strongly connected to the inner circles of government but not having ever been part of it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201775_202-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201775-202">[202]</a></sup> The Dutch right-wing populist [Geert Wilders](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geert_Wilders "Geert Wilders") had for example been a prominent back-bench MP for many years before launching his populist [Party for Freedom](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Party_for_Freedom "Party for Freedom"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774_190-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774-190">[190]</a></sup> while in South Africa, Malema had been leader of the governing [African National Congress](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_National_Congress "African National Congress") (ANC) youth league until he was expelled, at which he launched his own populist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEResnick2017111_203-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEResnick2017111-203">[203]</a></sup> Only a few populist leaders are "insiders", individuals who have held leading roles in government prior to portraying themselves as populists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201775–76_204-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201775%E2%80%9376-204">[204]</a></sup> One example is Thaksin Shinawatra, who was twice deputy prime minister of Thailand before launching his own populist political party;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201775–76_204-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201775%E2%80%9376-204">[204]</a></sup> another is [Rafael Correa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rafael_Correa "Rafael Correa"), who served as the Ecuadorean finance minister before launching a left-wing populist challenge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774_190-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201774-190">[190]</a></sup>

Some populist leaders give their name to wider populist political movements; examples include the [Peronism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peronism "Peronism") of [Juan Perón](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juan_Per%C3%B3n "Juan Perón") or the Fortuynism of [Pim Fortuyn](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pim_Fortuyn "Pim Fortuyn").

Populist leaders are sometimes also characterised as [strongmen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strongman_(politics) "Strongman (politics)") or—in Latin American countries—as [caudillos](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caudillo "Caudillo").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763_205-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763-205">[205]</a></sup> In a number of cases, such as Argentina's Perón or Venezuela's Chávez, these leaders have military backgrounds which contribute to their strongman image.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763_205-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763-205">[205]</a></sup> Other populist leaders have also evoked the strongman image without having a military background; these include Italy's Berlusconi, Slovakia's Mečiar, and Thailand's [Thaksin Shinawatra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thaksin_Shinawatra "Thaksin Shinawatra").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763_205-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763-205">[205]</a></sup> Populism and strongmen are not intrinsically connected, however; as stressed by Mudde and Rovira Kaltwasser, "only a minority of strongmen are populists and only a minority of populists is a strongman".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763_205-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763-205">[205]</a></sup> Rather than being populists, many strongmen—such as Spain's Francisco Franco—were elitists who led authoritarian administrations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763_205-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201763-205">[205]</a></sup>

In most cases, these populist leaders built a political organisation around themselves, typically a political party, although in many instances these remain dominated by the leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743–44_206-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743%E2%80%9344-206">[206]</a></sup> These individuals often give a populist movement its political identity, as is seen with movements like [Fortuynism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fortuynism "Fortuynism") in the Netherlands, [Peronism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peronism "Peronism") in Argentina, [Berlusconism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berlusconism "Berlusconism") in Italy and [Chavismo](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chavismo "Chavismo") in Venezuela.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743_183-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201743-183">[183]</a></sup> Populist mobilisation is not however always linked to a charismatic leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201742_207-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201742-207">[207]</a></sup> Mudde and Rovira Kaltwasser suggested that populist personalist leadership was more common in countries with a presidential system rather than a parliamentary one because these allow for the election of a single individual to the role of head of government without the need for an accompanying party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201758_208-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201758-208">[208]</a></sup> Examples where a populist leader has been elected to the presidency without an accompanying political party have included Peron in Argentina, Fujimori in Peru, and Correa in Ecuador.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201758_208-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201758-208">[208]</a></sup>

#### Media [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Media")

Further information on the role of the mass media in the emergence of populism: [Mediatization (media)](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediatization_(media) "Mediatization (media)")

A subset of populism which deals with the use of media by politicians is called "media populism".<sup id="cite_ref-209"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>

Populist leaders often use the media in order to mobilize their support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovira_KaltwasserTaggartOchoa_EspejoOstiguy2019270_212-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovira_KaltwasserTaggartOchoa_EspejoOstiguy2019270-212">[212]</a></sup> In Latin America, there is a long tradition of using mass media as a way for charismatic leaders to directly communicate with the poorly educated masses, first by radio and then by television.<sup id="cite_ref-213"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> The former Venezuelan President Hugo Chávez had a weekly show called _[Aló Presidente](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al%C3%B3_Presidente "Aló Presidente")_, which according to historian [Enrique Krauze](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enrique_Krauze "Enrique Krauze") gave some Venezuelans "at least the appearance of contact with power, through his verbal and visual presence, which may be welcomed by people who have spent most of their lives being ignored."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_214-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:0-214">[214]</a></sup>

The media has also been argued to have helped populists in countries of other regions by giving exposure to the most controversial politicians for commercial reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovira_KaltwasserTaggartOchoa_EspejoOstiguy2019467_215-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovira_KaltwasserTaggartOchoa_EspejoOstiguy2019467-215">[215]</a></sup> [Donald Trump](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") was claimed to have received $5 billion worth of free coverage during his 2016 campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-216"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> [Tabloids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tabloid_journalism "Tabloid journalism") are often stereotyped as presenting a platform for populist politics due to their tendency toward melodrama, infotainment, and conflict, and thus provide support for populist parties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkkerman2011932_217-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkkerman2011932-217">[217]</a></sup> Examples of this have been the support given by _[Kronen Zeitung](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kronen_Zeitung "Kronen Zeitung")_ to the Austrian Freedom Party and the Berlusconi-owned presses' support for Italy's [National Alliance](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Alliance_(Italy) "National Alliance (Italy)") in the mid-1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkkerman2011932_217-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkkerman2011932-217">[217]</a></sup> Based on his analysis of Dutch and British media, Tjitske Akkerman however argued that tabloids were no more prone to populism than the [quality press](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_press "Quality press").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkkerman2011942_218-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkkerman2011942-218">[218]</a></sup>

In the 21st century, populists have increasingly used [social media](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_media "Social media") to bypass the mainstream media and directly approach their target audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStierPoschBleierStrohmaier20171365_219-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStierPoschBleierStrohmaier20171365-219">[219]</a></sup> In earlier periods, before radio, thought "mass media" newspapers tended to operate more like social media than modern newspapers, publishing local gossip and with little fact-checking; the expansion of newspapers to rural areas of the United States in the early tweenith century increased support for populist partied and positions.<sup id="cite_ref-220"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> It has been claimed that while traditional media, acting as so-called 'gatekeepers', filter the messages that they broadcast through journalistic norms, social media permits a 'direct linkage' from political actors to potential audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-221"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> It has been claimed that the use of [Twitter](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twitter "Twitter") helped Donald Trump win the US presidency,<sup id="cite_ref-222"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> while the same has been claimed regarding the use of [YouTube](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/YouTube "YouTube") by the [Jair Bolsonaro 2018 presidential campaign](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jair_Bolsonaro_2018_presidential_campaign "Jair Bolsonaro 2018 presidential campaign").<sup id="cite_ref-223"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>

#### Electoral systems [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Electoral systems")

Political systems with low [political efficacy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_efficacy "Political efficacy") or high [wasted votes](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wasted_vote "Wasted vote") can contribute to populism.<sup id="cite_ref-224"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> Populist leaders have been claimed to be more successful in [presidential systems](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidential_system "Presidential system"). This is because such systems give advantage to charismatic populist leaders, especially when institutionalized parties are weak.<sup id="cite_ref-225"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> This is especially the case in two-round systems, because outsiders who might not win most votes in the first round of voting might be able to do so when faced against a mainstream candidate in the second round.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_226-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:1-226">[226]</a></sup> This has been claimed to be evident in the [1990 Peruvian general election](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1990_Peruvian_general_election "1990 Peruvian general election") won by [Alberto Fujimori](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alberto_Fujimori "Alberto Fujimori"), who lost on the first round.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_226-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:1-226">[226]</a></sup> Furthermore, [Juan José Linz](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juan_Jos%C3%A9_Linz "Juan José Linz") has argued that the direct relationship between the president and the electorate fosters a populist perception of the president as representing the whole people and their opponents as resisting the popular will.<sup id="cite_ref-227"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup>

### Political parties [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Political parties")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Acto_de_Posesion_del_Gobernador_Arias_Cardenas.jpg)

A 2012 rally by members of the left-wing populist United Socialist Party of Venezuela in [Maracaibo](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maracaibo "Maracaibo")

Another form of mobilisation is through populist political parties. Populists are not generally opposed to political representation, but merely want their own representatives, those of "the people", in power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201751_228-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201751-228">[228]</a></sup> In various cases, non-populist political parties have transitioned into populist ones;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201754–55HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser201910_229-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201754%E2%80%9355HawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser201910-229">[229]</a></sup> the elitist [Socialist Unity Party of Germany](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_Unity_Party_of_Germany "Socialist Unity Party of Germany"), a Marxist–Leninist group which governed [East Germany](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic "German Democratic Republic"), later transitioned after German re-unification into a populist party, [The Left](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Left_(Germany) "The Left (Germany)").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201754–55_230-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201754%E2%80%9355-230">[230]</a></sup> In other instances, such as the Austrian FPÖ and Swiss SVP, a non-populist party can have a populist faction which later takes control of the whole party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201755_231-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201755-231">[231]</a></sup>

In some examples where a political party has been dominated by a single charismatic leader, the latter's death has served to unite and strengthen the party, as with Argentina's [Justicialist Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Justicialist_Party "Justicialist Party") after Juan Perón's death in 1974, or the [United Socialist Party of Venezuela](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Socialist_Party_of_Venezuela "United Socialist Party of Venezuela") after Chávez's death in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756_232-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756-232">[232]</a></sup> In other cases, a populist party has seen one strong centralising leader replace another, as when [Marine Le Pen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marine_Le_Pen "Marine Le Pen") replaced her father Jean-Marie as the leader of the National Front in 2011, or when [Heinz-Christian Strache](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinz-Christian_Strache "Heinz-Christian Strache") took over from Haider as chair of the Freedom Party of Austria in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201755–56_233-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201755%E2%80%9356-233">[233]</a></sup>

Many populist parties achieve an electoral breakthrough but then fail to gain electoral persistence, with their success fading away at subsequent elections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201760_234-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201760-234">[234]</a></sup> In various cases, they are able to secure regional strongholds of support but with little support elsewhere in the country; the [Alliance for the Future of Austria](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alliance_for_the_Future_of_Austria "Alliance for the Future of Austria") (BZÖ) for instance gained national representation in the Austrian parliament solely because of its strong support in [Carinthia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carinthia "Carinthia").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201760_234-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201760-234">[234]</a></sup> Similarly, the Belgian [Vlaams Belang](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vlaams_Belang "Vlaams Belang") party has its stronghold in [Antwerp](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antwerp "Antwerp"), while the [Swiss People's Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swiss_People%27s_Party "Swiss People's Party") has its stronghold in [Zürich](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich "Zürich").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201760_234-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201760-234">[234]</a></sup>

### [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Social movements")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Madrid_-_Acampada_Sol_2011_43.JPG)

"Hear the wrath of the people", a member of the Indignados, a Spanish left-wing populist movement, in [Puerta del Sol](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Puerta_del_Sol "Puerta del Sol"), 2011

An additional form is that of the populist social movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser201910_235-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser201910-235">[235]</a></sup> Populist social movements are comparatively rare, as most [social movements](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_movement "Social movement") focus on a more restricted social identity or issue rather than identifying with "the people" more broadly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201751_228-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201751-228">[228]</a></sup> However, after the Great Recession of 2007 a number of populist social movements emerged, expressing public frustrations with national and international economic systems. These included the [Occupy movement](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupy_movement "Occupy movement"), which originated in the US and used the slogan "We are the 99%", and the Spanish [Indignados movement](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-austerity_movement_in_Spain "Anti-austerity movement in Spain"), which employed the motto: "real democracy now—we are not goods in the hands of politicians and bankers".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201748_236-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201748-236">[236]</a></sup>

Few populist social movements survive for more than a few years, with most examples, like the Occupy movement, petering out after their initial growth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756_232-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756-232">[232]</a></sup> In some cases, the social movement fades away as a strong leader emerges from within it and moves into electoral politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756_232-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756-232">[232]</a></sup> An example of this can be seen with the [India Against Corruption](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/India_Against_Corruption "India Against Corruption") social movement, from which emerged [Arvind Kejriwal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arvind_Kejriwal "Arvind Kejriwal"), who founded the [Aam Aadmi Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aam_Aadmi_Party "Aam Aadmi Party") ("Common Man Party").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756_232-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756-232">[232]</a></sup> Another is the Spanish Indignados movement which appeared in 2011 before spawning the [Podemos](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Podemos_(Spanish_political_party) "Podemos (Spanish political party)") party led by [Pablo Iglesias Turrión](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pablo_Iglesias_Turri%C3%B3n "Pablo Iglesias Turrión").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756–57Tormey2018266–67_237-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201756%E2%80%9357Tormey2018266%E2%80%9367-237">[237]</a></sup> These populist social movements can exert a broader societal impact which results in populist politicians emerging to prominence; the Tea Party and Occupy movements that appeared in the US during the late 2000s and early 2010s have been seen as an influence on the rise of Donald Trump and [Bernie Sanders](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernie_Sanders "Bernie Sanders") as prominent figures in the mid-2010s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser201911_238-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawkinsRovira_Kaltwasser201911-238">[238]</a></sup>

Some populist leaders have sought to broaden their support by creating supporter groups within the country. Chavez, for instance, ordered the formation of Bolivarian Circles, Communal Councils, Urban Land Committees, and Technical Water Roundtables across Venezuela.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_la_Torre2017205_239-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_la_Torre2017205-239">[239]</a></sup> These could improve political participation among poorer sectors of Venezuelan society, although also served as networks through which the state transferred resources to those neighbourhoods which produced high rates of support for Chavez government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_la_Torre2017205_239-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_la_Torre2017205-239">[239]</a></sup>

## Other themes [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Other themes")

### Democracy [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Populism&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Democracy")

Populism is a flexible term as it can be seen to exist in both democracies as well as authoritarian regimes.<sup id="cite_ref-Gherghina_Mişcoiu_Soare_2013_240-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Gherghina_Mi%C5%9Fcoiu_Soare_2013-240">[240]</a></sup> There have been intense debates about the relationship between populism and democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201779ZasloveGeurkinkJacobsAkkerman2021_241-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201779ZasloveGeurkinkJacobsAkkerman2021-241">[241]</a></sup> Some regard populism as being an intrinsic danger to democracy; others regard it as the only "true" form of democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanovan2004244MuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201779_242-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanovan2004244MuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201779-242">[242]</a></sup> Populists often present themselves as "true democrats".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell20084_59-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell20084-59">[59]</a></sup> It could be argued that populism is democratic as it allows voters to remove governments they do not approve via the ballot box because voting is an essential value for a state to be considered a democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-243"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> Albertazzi and McDonnell stated that populism and democracy were "inextricably linked",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell200810_244-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertazziMcDonnell200810-244">[244]</a></sup> the political scientist Manuel Anselmi described populism as being "deeply connected with democracy",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnselmi20182_245-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnselmi20182-245">[245]</a></sup> and March suggested that populism represented a "critique of democracy, not an alternative to it".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarch200773_246-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarch200773-246">[246]</a></sup> Mudde and Rovira Kaltwasser write that "In a world that is dominated by democracy and liberalism, populism has essentially become an illiberal democratic response to undemocratic liberalism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017116_247-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser2017116-247">[247]</a></sup> Adamidis argues that the effect of populism on democracy can be measured by reference to its impact on the democratic legal systems and, in particular, to the changes it effects on their rule of recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-248"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup>

Populism can serve as a democratic corrective by contributing to the mobilisation of social groups who feel excluded from political decision making.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarch200772MuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201783,_84_250-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarch200772MuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201783,_84-250">[250]</a></sup> It can also raise awareness among the socio-political elites of popular concerns in society, even if it makes the former uncomfortable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarch200772–73_251-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarch200772%E2%80%9373-251">[251]</a></sup> When some populists have taken power—most notably, Chávez in Venezuela—they have enhanced the use of direct democracy through the regular application of referendums.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201793–94_252-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201793%E2%80%9394-252">[252]</a></sup> For this reason, some democratic politicians have argued that they need to become more populist: [René Cuperus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Cuperus&action=edit&redlink=1 "René Cuperus (page does not exist)") of the [Dutch Labour Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dutch_Labour_Party "Dutch Labour Party") for instance called for [social democracy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_democracy "Social democracy") to become "more 'populist' in a leftist way" in order to engage with voters who felt left behind by cultural and technological change.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarch200773_246-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarch200773-246">[246]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Referendum_2016.jpg)

Hungarian Prime Minister [Viktor Orban](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viktor_Orban "Viktor Orban") has been cited as a populist leader who has undermined liberal democracy upon taking power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201791_253-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201791-253">[253]</a></sup>

Mudde and Rovira Kaltwasser argued that "populism is essentially democratic, but at odds with _liberal_ democracy," since populism is based on putting into effect "the will of the people". It is therefore majoritarian in nature, and opposed to the safeguarding of minority rights, which is a defining feature of liberal democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201790–81_254-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201790%E2%80%9381-254">[254]</a></sup> Populism also undermines the tenets of liberal democracy by rejecting notions of pluralism and the idea that anything, including constitutional limits, should constrain the "general will" of "the people".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarch200773MuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201781McDonnellCabrera2019493_255-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarch200773MuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201781McDonnellCabrera2019493-255">[255]</a></sup> In this, populist governance can lead to what the liberal philosopher [John Stuart Mill](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill "John Stuart Mill") described as the "[tyranny of the majority](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyranny_of_the_majority "Tyranny of the majority")".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarch200773_246-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarch200773-246">[246]</a></sup>

Populists tend to view democratic institutions as alienating,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkkerman200356_256-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkkerman200356-256">[256]</a></sup> and in practice, populists operating in liberal democracies have often criticised the independent institutions designed to protect the fundamental rights of minorities, particularly the judiciary and the media.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201781_257-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201781-257">[257]</a></sup> Berlusconi for instance criticised the Italian judiciary for defending the rights of communists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201781_257-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201781-257">[257]</a></sup> In countries like Hungary, Ecuador, and Venezuela, populist governments have curtailed the independent media.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201782_258-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuddeRovira_Kaltwasser201782-258">[258]</a></sup> Minorities have often suffered as a result.
