Another factor is that in the [post-Cold War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-Cold_War "Post-Cold War") period, liberal democracies no longer had the one-party states of the Eastern Bloc against which to favourably compare themselves; citizens were therefore increasingly able to compare the realities of the liberal democratic system with theoretical models of democracy, and find the former wanting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004555_395-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004555-395">[395]</a></sup> There is also the impact of [globalisation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globalisation "Globalisation"), which is seen as having seriously limited the powers of national elites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004555–56_396-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004555%E2%80%9356-396">[396]</a></sup> Such factors undermine citizens' belief in the competency of governing elite, opening up space for [charismatic leadership](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charismatic_authority "Charismatic authority") to become increasingly popular; although charismatic leadership is not the same as populist leadership, populists have been the main winners of this shift towards charismatic leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMudde2004556_394-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMudde2004556-394">[394]</a></sup> Peter Wilkins has argued that "The end of history and the post-Cold War extension and deepening of capitalism are central to understanding the rise of contemporary populist movements."<sup id="cite_ref-397"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-397">[397]</a></sup>

[Pippa Norris](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pippa_Norris "Pippa Norris") and [Ronald Inglehart](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Inglehart "Ronald Inglehart") connect economic and sociocultural theories of the causes of support for the growing populist movements in Western societies. The first theory they examine is the economic insecurity perspective which focuses on the consequences created by a transforming contemporary workforce and society in [post-industrial economies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-industrial_economy "Post-industrial economy"). Norris suggests that events such as globalisation, China's membership of the [World Trade Organisation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Trade_Organization "World Trade Organization") and cheaper imports have left the unsecured members of society (low-waged unskilled workers, single parents, the long term unemployed and the poorer white populations) seeking populist leaders such as [Donald Trump](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") and [Nigel Farage](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nigel_Farage "Nigel Farage").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInglehartNorris20161-229–30Bibliography_398-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInglehartNorris20161-229%E2%80%9330Bibliography-398">[398]</a></sup>

The other theory is the cultural backlash thesis, in which Norris and Inglehart suggest that the rise of populism is a reaction from previously dominant sectors of the population, the white, uneducated, elderly men of today, who feel threatened and marginalised by the progressive values of modern society. These groups in particular have a growing resentment towards their traditional values being scolded as politically incorrect and are much more likely to become supportive of anti-establishment, xenophobic political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInglehartNorris20161-229–30Bibliography_398-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInglehartNorris20161-229%E2%80%9330Bibliography-398">[398]</a></sup> Norris and Inglehart have analyzed data from the [World Values Survey](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Values_Survey "World Values Survey"). On this basis, they argue that while the [proximate cause](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proximate_cause "Proximate cause") of right-wing populist voting may be identified in sociocultural grievances, such grievances are increasingly being driven by economic insecurity and the erosion of traditional values.<sup id="cite_ref-Berman_3-19"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Berman-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norris_399-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-Norris-399">[399]</a></sup>

Using Brexit and Trump's election as examples, [Michael Sandel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Sandel "Michael Sandel") in his 2020 book _The Tyranny of Merit_ argues that populism came out of disenchantment with 'meritocratic' elites ruling over disenchanted working people.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_400-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:3-400">[400]</a></sup> He states the popular backlack against meriocracy predicted by [Michael Dunlop Young](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Dunlop_Young "Michael Dunlop Young") in [The Rise of the Meritocracy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Rise_of_the_Meritocracy "The Rise of the Meritocracy") to occur in the 2030s in fact arrived a few decades early.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_400-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:3-400">[400]</a></sup> Sandel suggests political systems that reject meriocracy and champion the [dignity of labour](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dignity_of_labour "Dignity of labour") as the solution to this problem.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_400-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populism#cite_note-:3-400">[400]</a></sup>
